  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_b84867d1-6a0a-4686-bbd3-c394d6d907da'
      });

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer9405165568592748065"><em>The Marbled Swarm</em> is Dennis Cooper’s most haunting work to date. In secret passageways, hidden rooms, and the troubled mind of our narrator, a mystery perpetually takes shape—and the most compelling clue to its final nature is “the marbled swarm” itself, a complex amalgam of language passed down from father to son.<br/><br/>Cooper ensnares the reader in a world of appearances, where</span>
  <span id="freeText9405165568592748065" style="display:none"><em>The Marbled Swarm</em> is Dennis Cooper’s most haunting work to date. In secret passageways, hidden rooms, and the troubled mind of our narrator, a mystery perpetually takes shape—and the most compelling clue to its final nature is “the marbled swarm” itself, a complex amalgam of language passed down from father to son.<br /><br />Cooper ensnares the reader in a world of appearances, where the trappings of high art, old money, and <em>haute cuisine</em> obscure an unspeakable system of coercion and surrender. And as the narrator stalks an elusive truth, traveling from the French countryside to Paris and back again, the reader will be seduced by a voice only Dennis Cooper could create.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9405165568592748065" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer221935724" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16207887084216020162">I love Dennis Cooper more than his characters love underage rough trade psycopath nymphet boytoys with daddy issues. But one thing Dennis Cooper is not is subtle. Sophisticated, yes; layered with meaning, absolutely -- but understated, no way. In fact, I would argue that his greatest talent is his ability to be the opposite of subtle: it's his unrelenting repulsiveness that so powerfully drives his work to ever crueler, ever more captivating heights. <br/><br/>Which is to say that I hated this book. I mea</span>
  <span id="freeText16207887084216020162" style="display:none">I love Dennis Cooper more than his characters love underage rough trade psycopath nymphet boytoys with daddy issues. But one thing Dennis Cooper is not is subtle. Sophisticated, yes; layered with meaning, absolutely -- but understated, no way. In fact, I would argue that his greatest talent is his ability to be the opposite of subtle: it's his unrelenting repulsiveness that so powerfully drives his work to ever crueler, ever more captivating heights. <br /><br />Which is to say that I hated this book. I mean, really? The ferocious and brilliant author of the violently enjoyable <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/51587.The_Sluts" rel="nofollow noopener">The Sluts</a> decides to pen a subtle, understated, meandering novel about a subtle, understated, meandering way of speaking, all of it a subtle, understated, meandering pastiche of the limp mid-century <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nouveau_roman" rel="nofollow noopener">nouveau roman</a> style? Yo, Dennis, Imma let you finish, but Paul Auster is the best Robbe-Grillet rip-off artist of all time. Of all time! And guess what, man: EVERYONE HATES PAUL AUSTER. <br /><br />Listen, I know Cooper's whole shtick is that he fucks with the reader's every last conception, but he fucked this one up in completely the wrong way. About 1/3 of the way through this mercifully brief novel the reader learns that if he feels confusion while reading, that is the intended response. For me, the issue with this clever conceit is that, while I didn't understand one lick of what was going on, more importantly, I also didn't give a flying fuck. It was just one empty and lifeless red herring-filled set piece after another, peopled with ciphers who made me yawn with boredom as they were disemboweled through their anuses or devoured bite by bite down to their toenails. I really hope this is just a one-off for Cooper and not a new pathway for his work, knocked out of a side wall like a secret passage in a crumbling <i>chateau</i>. I want the old sick and twisted Dennis back, and fast.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16207887084216020162" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1563801004" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1563801004" />
    <a title="Regan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1973082-regan"><img alt="Regan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1377986358p2/1973082.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1563801004?book_show_action=true">Feb 28, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Regan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Regan" href="/user/show/1973082-regan">Regan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1563801004" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9372152000234225182">The rating I’ve given may pique interest in this book, but caveats are incredibly necessary. If you are someone who needs to identify with the narrator or characters in a novel, <i>do not read this book.</i> If you are a reader who resents being intentionally manipulated by an author, <i>do not read this book.</i> If you are a reader who believes there is not a single thing of value in the works of Marquis de Sade, <i>do not read this book.</i> If you are a reader who cannot tolerate 48 pages of hints, redactions, c</span>
  <span id="freeText9372152000234225182" style="display:none">The rating I’ve given may pique interest in this book, but caveats are incredibly necessary. If you are someone who needs to identify with the narrator or characters in a novel, <i>do not read this book.</i> If you are a reader who resents being intentionally manipulated by an author, <i>do not read this book.</i> If you are a reader who believes there is not a single thing of value in the works of Marquis de Sade, <i>do not read this book.</i> If you are a reader who cannot tolerate 48 pages of hints, redactions, circumlocutions, and subterfuge, again <i>do not read this book.</i> However if you happen to be a person who fantasizes about raping and killing young boys, or if you are a depressed Emo kid fantasizing about being raped and eaten Hannibal Lecter style, then....um...PLEASE read this book rather than act on your inclinations. I say that with a shudder that I’d contend shakes the very ground, but I also live in Oklahoma. <br /><br />If you remain intrepid despite these warnings, you will find a book of fearfully obnoxious &amp; utterly awesome, terrifying prose. At 48 pages you will be rewarded, if that's the word for a rather dubious honor. At the very least, then the title &amp; the source of your frustration will be revealed.<br /><br />I’d like to be clear, I do not think there is anything wrong with drawing lines with regards to narratives or perspectives you’re willing to entertain. It is important that people draw these lines. For better or worse, I do not draw these kinds of lines, at least not all the time. While I’m an ethicist IRL, I don’t believe art is beholden to morality, and so I sometimes read depraved things and (very) occasionally I am awed by them.<br /><br />For example, Marquis de Sade awed me at a not-quite-young, but tender age; I was shocked that he could still shock after 200 some-odd years. I didn’t think it possible that I could be shocked given the cultural morass in which I found myself 15 years ago (and still do to tell the truth). I thought I’d seen pretty much all of it. I hadn’t.<br /><br />If you are unfamiliar with MDS then please turn there first. I do not find it accidental that this rapefest of a novel is set in France. If you are familiar with MDS and <i>120 Days of Sodom</i>, Dennis Cooper’s <i>The Marbled Swarm</i> is a close, but more spectacular (to choose as neutral of a word as possible) analogue. <br /><br />But if you <i>are</i> familiar with 120 Days of Sodom, you also probably know the scroll of the manuscript was lost (&amp; found) during the storming of the Bastille in 1784. You also probably know that the descriptions of the most depraved acts (the last month) are mere sketches, never completed. Cooper’s <i>The Marbled Swarm</i>, I believe, intends to flesh those scenes out in the most literal way imaginable.<br /><br />Imagine first, Brett Easton Ellis’s Patrick Bateman, but then imagine him without the shitty comical taste in 80s music, and with a predilection for Emo preteens. Imagine the debauchest version of a droll Whit Stillman film. In other words, imagine that wealth is maximally &amp; unapologetically corrupting.* Imagine that you believed (&amp; didn’t) in the ancient correspondence theory of truth whereby the existence of a thing--perhaps a lurid &amp; carnivorous inner yearning--is enough to make it real, or as imaginatively vivid &amp; fucked up as it must necessarily be to approximate your absolutely darkest and most perverse desires. To expose &amp; also purge them through the glaring exposure. That’s <i>The Marbled Swarm</i>. <br /><br />P.S. If you are neither a Sadist nor an Emo preteen, &amp; you are still interested in reading this novel, I advise you to set aside a day so as to spend as little time in this world as possible.<br /><br />*In the interest of full &amp; utterly disturbing disclosure, I began this review whilst still reading and I chose the “Imagine” trope without knowing that it would eventually surface in the novel’s repertoire of devices to lure the reader. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9372152000234225182" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_236865009" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/236865009" />
    <a title="Tosh" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/133661-tosh"><img alt="Tosh" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1538010402p2/133661.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/236865009?book_show_action=true">Nov 17, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tosh" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tosh" href="/user/show/133661-tosh">Tosh</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer236865009" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16940380719744928447">A very beautifully layered novel that one can almost taste the narrative. Considering it deals with cannibalism among other things this may not be your type of flavor - but it is an essential read by one of the great English language writers alive. What strikes me about the novel for me personally is the jaded aristocratic voice that runs through it. All of Dennis Cooper's novels have a strong visual sense - and usually with the minimal language. "The Marbled Swarm" is different because the text</span>
  <span id="freeText16940380719744928447" style="display:none">A very beautifully layered novel that one can almost taste the narrative. Considering it deals with cannibalism among other things this may not be your type of flavor - but it is an essential read by one of the great English language writers alive. What strikes me about the novel for me personally is the jaded aristocratic voice that runs through it. All of Dennis Cooper's novels have a strong visual sense - and usually with the minimal language. "The Marbled Swarm" is different because the text is so dense and beautifully spread out - that its just a joy to go over the sentences over and over again. It has its own music, and the images that come from the "music" is both funny and highly poetic.<br /><br />A lot of people will probably react to the violence and sex, but to me in the hands of M. Cooper its a beautiful instrument that plays a haunting melody. In about six months i am going to re-read this book - not only for the pleasure of the text, but also to dig into the narrative that is as twisted as the secret tunnels that are featured in this novel.</span>

<div id="review_236599303" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/236599303" />
    <a title="Nate D" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/406701-nate-d"><img alt="Nate D" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1203897989p2/406701.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/236599303?book_show_action=true">Nov 17, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nate D" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nate D" href="/user/show/406701-nate-d">Nate D</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Isabelle Adjani</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Nate D by: </span>
      Pierre Clementi
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/406701-nate-d?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/406701-nate-d?shelf=post-modernism">post-modernism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/406701-nate-d?shelf=read-in-2011">read-in-2011</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/406701-nate-d?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer236599303" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8290282769717204785"><i>The Marbled Swarm</i> is a performance of gruesome virtuosity, a blood-gilded house of cards that geometrically collapses down to a single card containing the superimposed forms of all that preceded it, at last finding itself reduced, pure and tragic, a simplicity it so desperately attempted to obscure with endless card tricks -- mysteries within mysteries, horrific acts minutely detailed, the ultimate veneer of language itself -- attempted to obscure out of nothing so much as failed self-preservati</span>
  <span id="freeText8290282769717204785" style="display:none"><i>The Marbled Swarm</i> is a performance of gruesome virtuosity, a blood-gilded house of cards that geometrically collapses down to a single card containing the superimposed forms of all that preceded it, at last finding itself reduced, pure and tragic, a simplicity it so desperately attempted to obscure with endless card tricks -- mysteries within mysteries, horrific acts minutely detailed, the ultimate veneer of language itself -- attempted to obscure out of nothing so much as failed self-preservation instincts.<br /><br />Reading this was a conflicting experience: pressing hard along the art/trash lines that post-modernism has gleefully attempted to erase, this is poetic, artful, densely layered and impeccably refined, yet also often willfully irredeemable and repugnant. And I don't say that lightly, I don't shock so easily anymore. Early on, we watch our protagonist's uncomfortable blackly funny attentions towards the young son of an aristocratic family whose supposedly haunted house he hopes to buy. But, he assures us, in the beginnings of the direct conversation that composes the entire book, he's not the ordinary predator we suspect. It's true, he's much worse, and will show us. But even then this isn't any sort of confessions-of-a-monster that it might be. As I said, it's a performance, our narrator spinning his complicated sentences in unexpected directions expressly for our amusement, entertainment, and revulsion, assuming our existence to such an extent that readership becomes a dialogue, or a game for two players. The utterly unique voice drives the entire text, binds together the dead ends, and incongruities, and eventually even invests the proceedings with unexpected ... well, to say more would be too much. But suffice to say that this narrator is one of the most fascinating I've encountered. If I hadn't just read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/780541.Island_People__American_Literature_Series_" title="Island People (American Literature Series) by Coleman Dowell" rel="nofollow noopener">Island People</a>, there'd be nothing close in recent memory, but in some ways these two are complimentary parts of a similar literary genius, hiding itself within its stories, within its very words.<br /><br /><blockquote>You who've read this far with any care and feel you know me to the point to which you've been empowered will have gleaned what I would like you to assume I felt when told my recent life had been the colophon of someone else's trail of bread crumbs.</blockquote><br /><br />Another note: many have reflected on the Robbe-Grillet-ness of parts of this, most obviously the text itself in one of its internal proxies, and in Cooper's direct thanks to Catherine Robbe-Grillet, but besides the more surface correspondences -- the uncertainties, secret rooms, the aestheticized horrors -- lies, perhaps, a truer similarity: Cooper, here, has concerned himself, on one level, with one of the great recurring Alain Robbe-Grillet themes: the act of writing itself.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8290282769717204785" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_343174730" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/343174730" />
    <a title="Daniel" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3977748-daniel"><img alt="Daniel" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1418943509p2/3977748.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/343174730?book_show_action=true">Jun 05, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Daniel" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Daniel" href="/user/show/3977748-daniel">Daniel</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3977748-daniel?shelf=lgbtq">lgbtq</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer343174730" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14372309638217873175">I just bought Dennis Cooper's <i>Closer</i> on my Kindle and I am already terrified that someone is going to look at my Kindle and start reading it and see that it is not only pornographic, but pornographic in the most disturbing sense possible. I am fully convinced that e-Readers were invented for the sole purpose of being able to hide all your erotica from your friends.<br/><br/>Dennis Cooper scares me, yet <i>The Marbled Swarm</i> was still one of the most interesting novels I've read this year. <i>The Marbled Swarm</i> re</span>
  <span id="freeText14372309638217873175" style="display:none">I just bought Dennis Cooper's <i>Closer</i> on my Kindle and I am already terrified that someone is going to look at my Kindle and start reading it and see that it is not only pornographic, but pornographic in the most disturbing sense possible. I am fully convinced that e-Readers were invented for the sole purpose of being able to hide all your erotica from your friends.<br /><br />Dennis Cooper scares me, yet <i>The Marbled Swarm</i> was still one of the most interesting novels I've read this year. <i>The Marbled Swarm</i> refers to the narrator's manner of speaking. It's a style that is both intricate and convoluted, but the most interesting thing about this <i>Marbled Swarm</i> is how it is also reflected in the plot itself: the plot continually stops, regresses, or goes off on a permanent tangent. The language is formal, and this formality is represented by a stunning lack of emotion in the narrator. Cannibalism, incest, rape, and abuse are revisited again and again as the plot circles around and around, attempting to avoid the whole point of the novel entirely. The events are terrifying and, in some ways, darkly funny. Yet it is an emotionless journey for the narrator, until the time comes when the heart of the novel cannot be avoided any longer.<br /><br />In short, it's <i>The Story of the Eye</i> for the 21st century. (Which I had no problem sharing with all my friends. I was too enthralled by my own deviousness.) This novel is hard to simplify and dismiss it as a gross-out novel, because there is some real pain here. Cooper is too intelligent to let himself be reduced so easily. I don't want to call it a masterwork, but it's damn brilliant.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14372309638217873175" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_155762129" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/155762129" />
    <a title="Jeff Jackson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/811567-jeff-jackson"><img alt="Jeff Jackson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1531248217p2/811567.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/155762129?book_show_action=true">Mar 21, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeff Jackson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeff Jackson" href="/user/show/811567-jeff-jackson">Jeff Jackson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer155762129" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6878639248026643544">Since this won’t be released for a while, I don’t want to say too much. Some quick thoughts: I'm a huge admirer of Dennis Cooper's work and The Marbled Swarm strikes me as a genuine masterpiece. Set in Parisian warehouse apartments and country chateaus, the novel is riddled with secret passages, doubles, cannibalism, and peepholes that reveal both more and less than they appear. Its labyrinthine structure is worthy of Robbe-Grillet and the puppetmaster narrator is an equal of Pale Fire’s Kinbote</span>
  <span id="freeText6878639248026643544" style="display:none">Since this won’t be released for a while, I don’t want to say too much. Some quick thoughts: I'm a huge admirer of Dennis Cooper's work and The Marbled Swarm strikes me as a genuine masterpiece. Set in Parisian warehouse apartments and country chateaus, the novel is riddled with secret passages, doubles, cannibalism, and peepholes that reveal both more and less than they appear. Its labyrinthine structure is worthy of Robbe-Grillet and the puppetmaster narrator is an equal of Pale Fire’s Kinbote in terms of his corrosive wit and disturbing half revelations. The sentences themselves are also exquisitely wrought, each one seeming to simultaneously expose and obscure some element of the narrative. <br /><br />This is Cooper’s most baroque and demanding book. If you’re new to his novels, I’d recommend reading My Loose Thread and/or God Jr before tackling this. Some goodwill and patience may be required to fully appreciate a novel that reshuffles itself with each chapter and delays its emotional wallop until the very last sentences. It’s also a book that gives up its mysteries slowly and I look forward to reading it again before the year’s out. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6878639248026643544" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_776095988" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/776095988" />

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/776095988?book_show_action=true">Nov 28, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Emma Sea" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Emma Sea" href="/user/show/5558652-emma-sea">Emma Sea</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5558652-emma-sea?shelf=auckland-library">auckland-library</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer776095988" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9980960192628326286">The book has me at a loss. Layers of story, inside an unreliable narrative, opening out into language, hidden within a secret, artificial tongue that is never used. The book exceeds my ability to comprehend or appreciate it, making any rating I may make pointless.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_679830562" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/679830562" />
    <a title="James" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/983671-james"><img alt="James" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/679830562?book_show_action=true">Jul 28, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="James" class="user" itemprop="url" name="James" href="/user/show/983671-james">James</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer679830562" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1094535291602336304">I just finished this.<br/><br/>Voyeurism, incest, molestation, pedophilia, child rape, murder, cannibalism...just another day at the office for Dennis Cooper.<br/><br/>If you've read his earlier works--for instance, the George Miles cycle--the disturbing themes should come as no surprise, but here his writing style is quite different. Whereas in his earlier works the style was flat, laconic, and minimalistic, here it is wordy and intentionally convoluted.<br/><br/>I think in the earlier books his characters didn't know how t</span>
  <span id="freeText1094535291602336304" style="display:none">I just finished this.<br /><br />Voyeurism, incest, molestation, pedophilia, child rape, murder, cannibalism...just another day at the office for Dennis Cooper.<br /><br />If you've read his earlier works--for instance, the George Miles cycle--the disturbing themes should come as no surprise, but here his writing style is quite different. Whereas in his earlier works the style was flat, laconic, and minimalistic, here it is wordy and intentionally convoluted.<br /><br />I think in the earlier books his characters didn't know how to communicate, and as a result spoke vaguely. Here, as befits a child of the Internet age, the narrator doesn't know how to communicate, and so speaks in a blathering torrent of words--practically a word salad--albeit one designed to hide, confuse, obfuscate, and trick.<br /><br />The plot is a Mobius strip. If you can get through the book and say definitively what was real and what was an elaborate lie, you're a better man than I am, Gunga Din. I'm still not entirely clear as to the ending, and I suspect that might be Cooper's whole point.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1094535291602336304" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer240884437" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1769318821864898753">I think its a novel about the French language, especially literary language, how seductive it once was and how it declined. And oddly enough is written is wonderfully capacious english. Dennis Cooper is a ####ing genius.<br/><br/>Literary French seduced a continent for several centuries, but its descendants have misheard it, in this account. And in any case the world now speaks English. So this is more an elegy for the 'marbled swarm' of FR writing, from Laclos and Sade to Rimbaud. The beginning of the en</span>
  <span id="freeText1769318821864898753" style="display:none">I think its a novel about the French language, especially literary language, how seductive it once was and how it declined. And oddly enough is written is wonderfully capacious english. Dennis Cooper is a ####ing genius.<br /><br />Literary French seduced a continent for several centuries, but its descendants have misheard it, in this account. And in any case the world now speaks English. So this is more an elegy for the 'marbled swarm' of FR writing, from Laclos and Sade to Rimbaud. The beginning of the end for the narrator is Bataille's Story of the Eye, not to mention Reage's Story of O, where it all becomes a parlour game.<br /><br />Actually, its all about parlours. Its a book about how interior literary French is, how it peeps through imaginary peep holes into drawing rooms and bed rooms. You could also read it as a letter (in English) addressed to Michel Houellebecq about how to write French!</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_1919090627" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1919090627" />
    <a title="Ben Manners" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/64633478-ben-manners"><img alt="Ben Manners" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1553653379p2/64633478.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1919090627?book_show_action=true">Feb 22, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ben Manners" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ben Manners" href="/user/show/64633478-ben-manners">Ben Manners</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1919090627" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3271639003349366594">The most horrific, offensive, depraved acts of (sexual) violence seem to wash over me and leave me unaffected when they're described so simply and matter of factly; drained of any emotion with no sense of impact or consequence for anyone involved. Not to mention the assumed willingness of the victims to engage in these extreme scenarios of BDSM – seemingly aware that they won't survive them – feels like a minor copout on the author's part (although I recognize and appreciate the darker interpret</span>
  <span id="freeText3271639003349366594" style="display:none">The most horrific, offensive, depraved acts of (sexual) violence seem to wash over me and leave me unaffected when they're described so simply and matter of factly; drained of any emotion with no sense of impact or consequence for anyone involved. Not to mention the assumed willingness of the victims to engage in these extreme scenarios of BDSM – seemingly aware that they won't survive them – feels like a minor copout on the author's part (although I recognize and appreciate the darker interpretation that the victims' consent - their alacrity even - is only presumed, as part of the perpetrators' larger delusions). And I "get it": the cold, smug detachment of our first-person narrator (whomever, whatever, whenever he actually is or isn't ) is part of the point. I'm just saying, as someone who feels comfortable saying he understands – and when executed creatively, respects – the mechanics of (for lack of a better term) "shock value", it doesn't shock or impress me when presented as a laundry list of "wouldn't it be fucked up if..." bullet-points scrawled out by a cluster of chuckling 8th graders at the back of a school bus. <br /><br />Not to insinuate that the sensational gore of the few actual acts of violence described in this book, or the consistent tone of inhumanity, are all it has to offer. <br />I went into this, my first Dennis Cooper after years of hearing about him, with no misconceptions of what I was getting into regarding the nature of its content, or how hallucinatory and intentionally confusing this particular book was (according to many other GoodReads reviews) ( and speaking of, that aforementioned "bloody octopus" line wasn't anywhere in The Marbled Swarm. I went back and checked – what's wrong with me? Most likely it's in another Cooper book. An understandable mistake seeing as that reviewer said he'd read 5 of Cooper's books in as many days, or something like that. Something to be said for pacing I guess). <br /><br />Because I went in prepared I read this one carefully, and I won't say my interest wasn't held throughout. And of course as I neared the end, feeling that I at least had a grasp on the twisting contradicting narrative; that it didn't actually need to make sense; remembering that at a few points it had been suggested the we the readers were complicit and involved somehow in this world, and that our reading The Marbled Swarm was a natural side-effect of being under the spell of the titular mind-control technique (I guess), and all the while someone was watching us from behind a far wall — well after all that the last chapter just makes sure it all unravels and nobody reading knows their bloody ass from their shattered elbow.<br /><br />But I was prepared for all that to some degree and look forward to reading other Dennis Cooper books, even re-reading this one at some point.<br /><br />(I'd like to mention that both the line where a boy's facial features are compared to the finger holes of a bowling ball, as well as, to paraphrase, the part when it's said of whatshisface by our narrator that he "resembled my brother if my brother had been a dirt trail that snails traveled across" both made me literally LOL. So see, it's not *all* incest and brutal rape-murders.)</span>
  <a data-text-id="3271639003349366594" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1089732266" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1089732266" />
    <a title="Glenn Conley" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/34812753-glenn-conley"><img alt="Glenn Conley" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1412894833p2/34812753.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1089732266?book_show_action=true">Oct 26, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Glenn Conley" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Glenn Conley" href="/user/show/34812753-glenn-conley">Glenn Conley</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/34812753-glenn-conley?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1089732266" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13416505777387202976">This book is just a long literary excuse to write about pedophilla, necrophilla, incest, and cannibalism. There is no real story in this book. <br/><br/>It's just one rich-ass nancy-boy of a narrator's account of the comings and goings of various relatives, groundskeepers, housekeepers, and whoever else may have passed by this strange chateau. And this house of torture just happens to have secret passages in the walls, where perverts can hang out and jack off. Like you do.<br/><br/>Then, there's the constant murmur</span>
  <span id="freeText13416505777387202976" style="display:none">This book is just a long literary excuse to write about pedophilla, necrophilla, incest, and cannibalism. There is no real story in this book. <br /><br />It's just one rich-ass nancy-boy of a narrator's account of the comings and goings of various relatives, groundskeepers, housekeepers, and whoever else may have passed by this strange chateau. And this house of torture just happens to have secret passages in the walls, where perverts can hang out and jack off. Like you do.<br /><br />Then, there's the constant murmurings of incest, rape, and torture that usually ends in the corpse being banged some more, then eaten in some lavish banquet. You know, typical rich people stuff. I'm sure Bill Gates does shit like this all the time.<br /><br />Oh, and did I mention that for some reason everyone is gay? Oh yea. Not like flaming gay. Well, except the narrator. He's a flaming faggot if I ever heard of one. I mean his narrative is written like its the 1700's or something. Like hes just such a fancy faggot that he has to talk like some goddamn slave owner. And then in the next sentence he's sending a text to someone, going on about the cute little slave boy he's just purchased.<br /><br />Now, I'm not gay bashing at all, mind you. Go ahead, be a faggot. Everyone loves a cocksucker. I know, I do. But I'm just saying, how does being such a goddamn faggot add to this story? It doesn't. Probably because there is no goddamn story. </span>
  <a data-text-id="13416505777387202976" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1173688274" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1173688274" />
    <a title="Robert Vaughan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2785489-robert-vaughan"><img alt="Robert Vaughan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1443967780p2/2785489.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1173688274?book_show_action=true">Jan 19, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Robert Vaughan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Robert Vaughan" href="/user/show/2785489-robert-vaughan">Robert Vaughan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2785489-robert-vaughan?shelf=books-i-read-in-2015">books-i-read-in-2015</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1173688274" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17400041620791759182">This was disturbing in the most strange and offensive ways. Especially as a gay man, I found it oddly unappealing, and also, as Cooper is one of my favorite authors (especially his early poems), I was disappointed. Still, for taking on such provocative material, the rare complexities and for the attempt of this novel about such violent things as cannibalism of young boys and more, I give it these stars.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3573774433" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3573774433" />
    <a title="Joaquín Guirao" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/40615544-joaqu-n-guirao"><img alt="Joaquín Guirao" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1531859408p2/40615544.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3573774433?book_show_action=true">Sep 30, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joaquín Guirao" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joaquín Guirao" href="/user/show/40615544-joaqu-n-guirao">Joaquín Guirao</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3573774433" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3631660343736016203">I really didn't get the point of this book. It's not like I'm a stranger to Dennis Cooper books, I read and enjoyed some of them! It's just like... well, this book was written, intentionally, as a bad imitation of the nouvelle roman sort of books (that are not my bag, that is true, also), but, why do that? It's like a seasoned musician writting an imitation of shitty punk and not coming up with good melodies and riffs. The whole book, also, falls flat on what the author intended to do, and got t</span>
  <span id="freeText3631660343736016203" style="display:none">I really didn't get the point of this book. It's not like I'm a stranger to Dennis Cooper books, I read and enjoyed some of them! It's just like... well, this book was written, intentionally, as a bad imitation of the nouvelle roman sort of books (that are not my bag, that is true, also), but, why do that? It's like a seasoned musician writting an imitation of shitty punk and not coming up with good melodies and riffs. The whole book, also, falls flat on what the author intended to do, and got to do in other books. I guess that he was presenting a flat world with flat characters (there is even a character that it is obssesed with sexualizing flattened (in a gory way) people), and get an emotion out of that, but I got nothing out of there. Just kind of creeped out by the whole pedophilia thing that Dennis Cooper is so obssesed with (he didn't even talk about it, and how destructive it is, it's more of a sadean thrill that's not even as extreme as Sade). Also, the whole garbled syntax thing wasn't as "inciting" as the author thought, just "kind of irritating but passable".<br /><br />I don't give the book one star because there are a couple of really great parts were the genius of Dennis Cooper brights, and the end was kind of ok. I mean, it was the best part of the book, and the only part that had the pathos that I love from Dennis Cooper, but if it was on Ugly Man (from now, my favourite Dennis Cooper book), it'd have been just kind of ok.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3631660343736016203" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1886023118" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1886023118" />
    <a title="Pham" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6756527-pham"><img alt="Pham" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1546497039p2/6756527.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1886023118?book_show_action=true">Aug 05, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Pham" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Pham" href="/user/show/6756527-pham">Pham</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1886023118" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6895474306343622154">I. Finally. Finished. This. Beast. Well it's not a beast because it's thick or grand or anything. It's a beast because as I read along, I kept having to back-track, double-check, self-doubt. The language was infuriatingly twisted, the plot - wait what, non-existent. Not to mention the long gap of time (a year, at least) that I've found myself wanting to just throw the book away, to leave it to rot. I have read only another from Dennis Cooper - Frisk - and while Frisk was disturbing and nauseatin</span>
  <span id="freeText6895474306343622154" style="display:none">I. Finally. Finished. This. Beast. Well it's not a beast because it's thick or grand or anything. It's a beast because as I read along, I kept having to back-track, double-check, self-doubt. The language was infuriatingly twisted, the plot - wait what, non-existent. Not to mention the long gap of time (a year, at least) that I've found myself wanting to just throw the book away, to leave it to rot. I have read only another from Dennis Cooper - Frisk - and while Frisk was disturbing and nauseating in its own way, The Marbled Swarm was incorrigible. I didn't know what I was getting into back when I picked this up. I still have no idea now. But is the sickening story making my eyes glued to the pages and wondering what the hell might happen next? Yes, fuck yes and I'm actually mad with myself for that haha. <br /><br />Mind you, The Marbled Swarm is not an entertainment before sleep. You do need to be very sober, very awake to at least fathom the hell is written down. I learned it the hard way.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6895474306343622154" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3539456914" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3539456914" />
    <a title="Dan Riaz" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/65204196-dan-riaz"><img alt="Dan Riaz" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1487636375p2/65204196.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3539456914?book_show_action=true">Sep 09, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dan Riaz" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dan Riaz" href="/user/show/65204196-dan-riaz">Dan Riaz</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3539456914" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1150750143635871302">One of the most unique novels I have ever read. <br/><br/>But agree with the other reviewers, this is definitely NOT for everyone. The subject matter is extremely transgressive and will put off most readers from finishing the book. <br/><br/>That said, if you're up for a dark adventure (which in the age of Jeffrey Epstein, maybe even received as a social commentary), the writing is brilliant...<br/><br/>I would sum up the novel up as journey into the twisted psyche of a sociopath...think Jeffrey Epstein meets Patrick Bateman</span>
  <span id="freeText1150750143635871302" style="display:none">One of the most unique novels I have ever read. <br /><br />But agree with the other reviewers, this is definitely NOT for everyone. The subject matter is extremely transgressive and will put off most readers from finishing the book. <br /><br />That said, if you're up for a dark adventure (which in the age of Jeffrey Epstein, maybe even received as a social commentary), the writing is brilliant...<br /><br />I would sum up the novel up as journey into the twisted psyche of a sociopath...think Jeffrey Epstein meets Patrick Bateman (from American Psycho), and you have the protagonist down...<br /><br />Now imagine the narrative told in the first-person, where the protagonist enjoys speaking to the reader and breaking the 4th wall...<br /><br />Definitely planning to read more Dennis Cooper, all I can say is wow...<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1150750143635871302" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('adVR0SJGWUS0pU77pbWbuu1wz+Kn7xOR5jBbzgNRBplKcNr5vn0R32bTNNS8QkU89bP79JIsa3xqYNSux4T9BQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('mX/LX7yqjj8WAdAgD+yo0EYtjueXIQ9lQYl8V9OZ5/+62kB3IJHGpMR3qg8WG3ZWXu668aLid4jN2fM3F0wcYw==')})
  }
//]]>

Burroughs<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p6\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p0\"><\/span><\/span> 3.59 avg rating &mdash; 11,929 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1985\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer17474624095912494144\">Escrita en 1952 pero no publicada hasta 1985 debido a su franca plasmación del deseo homosexual, \"Queer\", obra temprana de William S. Burroughs, es al mismo tiempo un descarnado autorretrato narrativo, una historia de amor brutalmente realista, una g<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText17474624095912494144\" style=\"display:none\">Escrita en 1952 pero no publicada hasta 1985 debido a su franca plasmación del deseo homosexual, &quot;Queer&quot;, obra temprana de William S. Burroughs, es al mismo tiempo un descarnado autorretrato narrativo, una historia de amor brutalmente realista, una grotesca fantasía tragicómica y una ingeniosa novela política. Un libro que proporciona muchas claves fundamentales para adentrarse en el arrollador universo literario del autor. Esta edición definitiva, editada con motivo del 25 aniversario de su primera publicación, incorpora una extensa y documentada introducción de Oliver Harris en la que se repasan las complejas circunstancias personales en las que la obra fue escrita y que marcarían la vida y la posterior trayectoria literaria de Burroughs. Y se incluye también, a modo de epílogo, el texto que el propio autor escribió como prólogo para la edición de 1985.\n\n&quot;Queer&quot; está ambientada en un inmenso suburbio, que Burroughs definiría más tarde como la «Interzona», y que abarca desde la Ciudad de México, capital mundial del delito, hasta Panamá. Un álter ego del escritor, Lee, teje su tela amorosa en torno a Allerton, un joven ambiguo, indiferente como un animal. Deambula por locales cada vez más sórdidos, en los que pulula una fauna en estado de descomposición, y en esas excursiones, como un pícaro alienado, nos regala astillas radiactivas de su negrísimo humor. Para resolver sus obsesiones mortíferas y sexuales, Lee parte con su amigo a la búsqueda de la ayahuasca, droga absoluta capaz de otorgar el control total sobre los cerebros, y por eso mismo codiciada por Rusia y Estados Unidos... y por todo adicto. Dispuesto a abismarse en todos los peligros, como un santo o un criminal con orden de búsqueda y captura, Lee no tiene nada que perder. En esta novela aflora por primera vez ese paisaje alucinado que hoy todo lector reconoce como el mundo particular de William S. Burroughs.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"17474624095912494144\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n    <\/div>\n\n      <div class=\'wtrButtonContainer Four boys becoming men: Juice Terry, the work-shy fanny-merchant, with corkscrew curls and sticky fingers; Billy the boxer: driven, controlled, playing to his strengths; Carl, the Milky Bar Kid, drifting along to his own soundtrack; and the doomed Gally - who has one less skin than everyone else and seems to find catastrophe at every corner. As we follow their lives from the seventies into the new century - from punk to techno, from speed to Es - we can see each of them trying to struggle out from under the weight of the conditioning of class and culture, peer pressure and their parents\' hopes that maybe their sons will do better than they did. What binds the four of them is the friendship formed by the scheme, their school, and their ambition to escape from both; their loyalty fused in street morality: back up your mates, don\'t hit women and, most importantly, never grass - on anyone. Despite its scale and ambition, Glue has all Irvine Welsh\'s usual pace and vigour, crackling dialogue, scabrous set-pieces and black, black humour, but it is also a grown-up book about growing up -       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/29064.Dennis_Cooper">About Dennis Cooper</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/29064.Dennis_Cooper"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1395593423p3/29064.jpg);'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer14559157308482897019">Dennis Cooper was born on January 10, 1953 and grew up in the Southern California cities of Covina and Arcadia. In 1976, he founded Little Caesar Magazine and Press, which he ran until 1982. In 1985, he moved to Amsterdam for two and a half years, where he began his ten year long project, The George Miles Cycle, an interconnected sequence of five novels that includes Closer, Frisk, Try, Guide, and</span>
  <span id="freeText14559157308482897019" style="display:none">Dennis Cooper was born on January 10, 1953 and grew up in the Southern California cities of Covina and Arcadia. In 1976, he founded Little Caesar Magazine and Press, which he ran until 1982. In 1985, he moved to Amsterdam for two and a half years, where he began his ten year long project, The George Miles Cycle, an interconnected sequence of five novels that includes Closer, Frisk, Try, Guide, and Period. His post-George Miles Cycle novels include My Loose Thread, The Sluts and his most recent novel, the highly acclaimed God, Jr. Other works include the short-story collection Wrong, a collection of poetry titled The Dream Police and All Ears: Cultural Cristicism, Essays and Obituaries. Dennis Cooper currently spends his time between Los Angeles and Paris. His latest work, a limited edition collection of new poems, titled The Weaklings is available now from Fanzine Press.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14559157308482897019" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
